The Four Kawns: Definitions and Classification
Defines the four kawns and their rational classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of kawn (sukun, haraka, iftiraq, ijtima') and how they are defined based on the occurrence of a substance in a place relative to another substance.

Core Idea
The four kawns are defined by the relative precedence of occurrences in places.

In this passage, the author enumerates the four types of kawn: stillness, motion, separation, and conjunction. He then explains the rational basis for this classification, deriving the definitions of stillness and motion from the relationship between the occurrence of a substance in a place and its previous occurrences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the four kawns listed at the beginning.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical structure.
2
Understand the classification scheme
Follow the author's reasoning: the occurrence of a substance in a place is considered either in relation to another substance or not. The latter is then divided based on precedence.
This is the key to understanding the definitions.
3
Grasp the definitions of sukun and haraka
Sukun is defined as a second occurrence in a first place; haraka as a first occurrence in a second place. Reflect on what 'first' and 'second' mean in this context.
These definitions rely on the concept of precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الكون أربعة هي السكون والحركة والافتراق والاجتماع، وذلك لأن حصول الجوهر في الحيز إما أن يعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر أو لا، والثاني وهو يعتبر بالقياس إلى جوهر آخر قسمان لأنه إن كان ذلك الحصول مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون، وإن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فحركة، وعلى هذا فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة حصول أول في حيز ثان. ويرد على الحصر على حصر القسم الثاني في الحركة والسكون الحصول في أول الحدوث أي حصول الجوهر في الحيز في أول زمان حدوثه فإنه كون غير مسبوق بكون آخر لا في ذلك الحيز ولا في حيز آخر، فلا يكون سكوناً ولا حركة، فذهب أبو الهذيل إلى بطلان الحصر، وقال: الجوهر في أول زمان حدوثه كائن لا متحرك ولا ساكن. وقال أبو هاشم وأتباعه: أنه أي الكون في أول الحدوث [يكون سكوناً]. قوله: (في حيز أول) أي غير مسبوق بحيز آخر بلا واسطة كما هو المتبادر سواء كان سابقاً على حيز آخر أو لا كالجسم الذي حصل في مكانه ولم ينتقل عنه، وكذا قوله حصول أول غير مسبوق بحصول آخر، سواء كان سابقاً على حصول آخر كما إذا عدم الجوهر بعد الحصول في الحيز الثاني أو لا. قوله: (إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون وإن كان مسبوقاً إلخ) أراد بالسبق في الموضعين السبق بالاتصال، وإلا فالجسم إذا تحرك من حيز إلى حيز ثم منه إلى الحيز الأول يصدق على الحصول الثاني أنه حصول مسبوق بحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه حركة لا سكون وإذا سكن الجسم بعد الحركة يصدق على حصوله الذي هو سكون أنه حصول مسبوق بالحصول في حيز آخر، وإن كان مسبوقاً بالحصول في ذلك الحيز أيضاً، ولو قال: إن اتصل بحصول سابق في حيز آخر فحركة وإلا فسكون لكان أظهر. قوله: (فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة إلخ) أولية الحيز في السكون لا يلزم أن يكون تحقيقياً بل قد يكون تقديرياً كما في الساكن الذي لا يتحرك قطعاً فلا يحصل في حيز ثان، وكذا أولية الحصول في الحركة لجواز أن ينعدم المتحرك في آن انقطاع الحركة فلا تتحقق له حصول ثان.
The types of kawn are four: stillness, motion, separation, and conjunction. That is because the occurrence of the substance in a place is either considered in relation to another substance or not, and the second is considered in relation to another substance in two divisions: if that occurrence is preceded by its occurrence in that same place, it is stillness; if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place, it is motion. According to this, stillness is a second occurrence in a first place, and motion is a first occurrence in a second place. An objection is raised against this division of the second division into motion and stillness: the occurrence at the first moment of origination, i.e., the occurrence of the substance in a place at the first time of its origination; it is a kawn that is not preceded by another kawn neither in that place nor in another place, so it is neither stillness nor motion. Thus Abu al-Hudhayl held that the division is invalid, saying: the substance at the first moment of its origination is occurring, not moving and not still. Abu Hashim and his followers said: it (i.e., the kawn at the first moment) [is stillness]. His statement: (in a first place) meaning not preceded by another place without mediation, as is evident, whether it is prior to another place or not, like a body that has settled in its place and not moved from it; likewise his statement: first occurrence not preceded by another occurrence, whether it is prior to another occurrence or not, as when the substance ceases to exist after occurrence in the second place or not. His statement: (if it is preceded by its occurrence in that same place it is stillness, and if preceded by its occurrence in another place...) He intended by 'preceded' in both instances immediate precedence; otherwise, when a body moves from a place to a place and then from there back to the first place, the second occurrence is truthfully described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in that same place, yet it is motion, not stillness. And when a body becomes still after motion, its occurrence which is stillness is truthfully described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in another place, even though it is also preceded by an occurrence in that same place. If he had said: if it is connected to a previous occurrence in another place it is motion, otherwise stillness, it would be clearer. His statement: (so stillness is a second occurrence in a first place and motion is a first occurrence in a second place...) The priority of the place in stillness does not have to be real, but may be estimated, as in the case of something still that never moves, so it does not occur in a second place. Likewise, the priority of the occurrence in motion may be that the moving thing ceases to exist at the instant the motion stops, so it does not have a second occurrence realized for it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of kawn (accident of being in a place) and addresses the problem of the first moment of existence. The author begins by stating that the types of kawn are four: sukun (stillness), haraka (motion), iftiraq (separation), and ijtima' (conjunction). He explains the basis for this classification: considering the occurrence (hushul) of a substance (jawhar) in a place either in relation to another substance or not; the second case (not in relation) is then divided based on whether the occurrence is preceded by an occurrence in the same place (then it is sukun) or in a different place (then it is haraka). Hence sukun is a second occurrence in a first place, and haraka is a first occurrence in a second place. The author then raises an objection: this division fails to account for the occurrence at the first moment of origination (awwal al-huduth), which is a kawn not preceded by any other kawn, so it is neither sukun nor haraka. He reports that Abu al-Hudhayl used this to deny the division, claiming the substance at that moment is occurring but neither moving nor still. Abu Hashim and his followers, however, considered it stillness. The commentary then elaborates on definitions: 'in a first place' means not preceded by another place without mediation, and 'first occurrence' means not preceded by another occurrence. It clarifies that 'preceded' means immediate precedence (al-sabq bi al-ittisal); otherwise, the definitions would fail, e.g., when a body returns to a previous place (that motion is still motion) or when stillness follows motion (that stillness is still stillness despite being preceded by an occurrence in another place). The commentator suggests a clearer formulation: 'if connected to a previous occurrence in another place, it is motion; otherwise stillness.' Finally, it notes that the priority of place in stillness and the priority of occurrence in motion may be estimated (taqdiri) rather than real, accounting for cases where a body never moves or ceases to exist after motion.

Key Terms
كون (kawn)
سكون (sukun)
حركة (haraka)
حصول (hushul)
جوهر (jawhar)
حيز (hayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing sukun from haraka?
A) Whether the substance is moving or still.
B) Whether the occurrence is preceded by an occurrence in the same place (sukun) or a different place (haraka).
C) Whether the occurrence is in relation to another substance.
D) Whether the occurrence is the first or second time the substance exists.

Key Takeaway
The four kawns are derived from considering the occurrence of a substance in a place relative to another substance or not, and then by precedence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a moment of stillness in your life, do you ever consider what preceded it? According to this lesson, stillness is a 'second occurrence in a first place'—how does that change the way you view your routines and habits? Are your moments of stillness truly a return to a previous state, or are they something new?
?
Think about a recent change you made—a 'motion' in your life. Was it truly a first occurrence in a new 'place', or was it merely a rearrangement of what was already there? This lesson defines motion as a first occurrence in a second place—what does that say about the nature of change and the need for genuine new beginnings?
?
The classification of kawns depends on considering a substance in relation to another or not. In your interactions with others, do you consider your actions as being in relation to them (separation or conjunction) or as independent? How might understanding the relational nature of kawn help you be more mindful of your impact on others?

The First Moment Problem and Its Resolution
Discusses the objection of the first moment and the responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the occurrence at the first moment of origination (awwal al-huduth) does not fit the definitions of sukun or haraka, and how scholars like Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Hashim responded.

Core Idea
The first moment of existence poses a problem for the definition of kawn, resolved by considering it sukun or denying the division.

Having defined sukun and haraka based on precedence, the author raises an important objection: what about the very first moment a substance comes into existence? Its occurrence is not preceded by any occurrence, so it cannot be sukun (which requires a previous occurrence in the same place) nor haraka (which requires a previous occurrence in another place). This leads to a disagreement between Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Hashim. The commentary then clarifies the definitions to accommodate this case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and identify why the occurrence at the first moment does not fit the definitions of sukun or haraka.
The key point is that it is not preceded by any kawn.
2
Compare the two responses
Note the positions of Abu al-Hudhayl (rejects the division) and Abu Hashim (considers it sukun).
Both responses are grounded in the same objection.
3
Understand the clarifications
The commentary explains the terms 'first place' and 'first occurrence', specifies that 'preceded' means immediate precedence, and introduces estimated priority.
These clarifications resolve potential counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الكون أربعة هي السكون والحركة والافتراق والاجتماع، وذلك لأن حصول الجوهر في الحيز إما أن يعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر أو لا، والثاني وهو يعتبر بالقياس إلى جوهر آخر قسمان لأنه إن كان ذلك الحصول مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون، وإن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فحركة، وعلى هذا فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة حصول أول في حيز ثان. ويرد على الحصر على حصر القسم الثاني في الحركة والسكون الحصول في أول الحدوث أي حصول الجوهر في الحيز في أول زمان حدوثه فإنه كون غير مسبوق بكون آخر لا في ذلك الحيز ولا في حيز آخر، فلا يكون سكوناً ولا حركة، فذهب أبو الهذيل إلى بطلان الحصر، وقال: الجوهر في أول زمان حدوثه كائن لا متحرك ولا ساكن. وقال أبو هاشم وأتباعه: أنه أي الكون في أول الحدوث [يكون سكوناً]. قوله: (في حيز أول) أي غير مسبوق بحيز آخر بلا واسطة كما هو المتبادر سواء كان سابقاً على حيز آخر أو لا كالجسم الذي حصل في مكانه ولم ينتقل عنه، وكذا قوله حصول أول غير مسبوق بحصول آخر، سواء كان سابقاً على حصول آخر كما إذا عدم الجوهر بعد الحصول في الحيز الثاني أو لا. قوله: (إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون وإن كان مسبوقاً إلخ) أراد بالسبق في الموضعين السبق بالاتصال، وإلا فالجسم إذا تحرك من حيز إلى حيز ثم منه إلى الحيز الأول يصدق على الحصول الثاني أنه حصول مسبوق بحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه حركة لا سكون وإذا سكن الجسم بعد الحركة يصدق على حصوله الذي هو سكون أنه حصول مسبوق بالحصول في حيز آخر، وإن كان مسبوقاً بالحصول في ذلك الحيز أيضاً، ولو قال: إن اتصل بحصول سابق في حيز آخر فحركة وإلا فسكون لكان أظهر. قوله: (فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة إلخ) أولية الحيز في السكون لا يلزم أن يكون تحقيقياً بل قد يكون تقديرياً كما في الساكن الذي لا يتحرك قطعاً فلا يحصل في حيز ثان، وكذا أولية الحصول في الحركة لجواز أن ينعدم المتحرك في آن انقطاع الحركة فلا تتحقق له حصول ثان.
The types of kawn are four: stillness, motion, separation, and conjunction. That is because the occurrence of the substance in a place is either considered in relation to another substance or not, and the second is considered in relation to another substance in two divisions: if that occurrence is preceded by its occurrence in that same place, it is stillness; if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place, it is motion. According to this, stillness is a second occurrence in a first place, and motion is a first occurrence in a second place. An objection is raised against this division of the second division into motion and stillness: the occurrence at the first moment of origination, i.e., the occurrence of the substance in a place at the first time of its origination; it is a kawn that is not preceded by another kawn neither in that place nor in another place, so it is neither stillness nor motion. Thus Abu al-Hudhayl held that the division is invalid, saying: the substance at the first moment of its origination is occurring, not moving and not still. Abu Hashim and his followers said: it (i.e., the kawn at the first moment) [is stillness]. His statement: (in a first place) meaning not preceded by another place without mediation, as is evident, whether it is prior to another place or not, like a body that has settled in its place and not moved from it; likewise his statement: first occurrence not preceded by another occurrence, whether it is prior to another occurrence or not, as when the substance ceases to exist after occurrence in the second place or not. His statement: (if it is preceded by its occurrence in that same place it is stillness, and if preceded by its occurrence in another place...) He intended by 'preceded' in both instances immediate precedence; otherwise, when a body moves from a place to a place and then from there back to the first place, the second occurrence is truthfully described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in that same place, yet it is motion, not stillness. And when a body becomes still after motion, its occurrence which is stillness is truthfully described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in another place, even though it is also preceded by an occurrence in that same place. If he had said: if it is connected to a previous occurrence in another place it is motion, otherwise stillness, it would be clearer. His statement: (so stillness is a second occurrence in a first place and motion is a first occurrence in a second place...) The priority of the place in stillness does not have to be real, but may be estimated, as in the case of something still that never moves, so it does not occur in a second place. Likewise, the priority of the occurrence in motion may be that the moving thing ceases to exist at the instant the motion stops, so it does not have a second occurrence realized for it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of kawn (accident of being in a place) and addresses the problem of the first moment of existence. The author begins by stating that the types of kawn are four: sukun (stillness), haraka (motion), iftiraq (separation), and ijtima' (conjunction). He explains the basis for this classification: considering the occurrence (hushul) of a substance (jawhar) in a place either in relation to another substance or not; the second case (not in relation) is then divided based on whether the occurrence is preceded by an occurrence in the same place (then it is sukun) or in a different place (then it is haraka). Hence sukun is a second occurrence in a first place, and haraka is a first occurrence in a second place. The author then raises an objection: this division fails to account for the occurrence at the first moment of origination (awwal al-huduth), which is a kawn not preceded by any other kawn, so it is neither sukun nor haraka. He reports that Abu al-Hudhayl used this to deny the division, claiming the substance at that moment is occurring but neither moving nor still. Abu Hashim and his followers, however, considered it stillness. The commentary then elaborates on definitions: 'in a first place' means not preceded by another place without mediation, and 'first occurrence' means not preceded by another occurrence. It clarifies that 'preceded' means immediate precedence (al-sabq bi al-ittisal); otherwise, the definitions would fail, e.g., when a body returns to a previous place (that motion is still motion) or when stillness follows motion (that stillness is still stillness despite being preceded by an occurrence in another place). The commentator suggests a clearer formulation: 'if connected to a previous occurrence in another place, it is motion; otherwise stillness.' Finally, it notes that the priority of place in stillness and the priority of occurrence in motion may be estimated (taqdiri) rather than real, accounting for cases where a body never moves or ceases to exist after motion.

Key Terms
أول الحدوث (awwal al-huduth)
سبق بالاتصال (sabq bi al-ittisal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the occurrence at the first moment is neither sukun nor haraka, thus invalidating the entire classification.
Correction: Abu Hashim considers it sukun, and the commentary shows it can be accommodated by understanding 'first place' and 'first occurrence' appropriately.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Abu al-Hudhayl reject the division of the second kawn into motion and stillness?
A) He thought motion and stillness are not opposites.
B) The occurrence at the first moment is not preceded by any kawn, so it cannot be motion or stillness.
C) He believed separation and conjunction are more fundamental.
D) He argued that all kawans are essentially the same.

Key Takeaway
The objection of the first moment is resolved by understanding the definitions of sukun and haraka in terms of immediate precedence and estimated priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own first moment of accountability (bulugh). Do you see it as a state of stillness (a fixed event) or motion (a continuous journey)? How does that perspective influence your current religious practice?
?
This lesson shows that precise definitions can resolve deep confusions. In what area of your faith or life have you accepted ambiguity because clarity would require difficult change? What would it cost you to seek that clarity today?

Rational Judgments

Objection: Motion Cannot Be Composed of Rests Because They Are Opposites
Presents the objection and its answer with a nuanced distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between motion from a space and motion to a space, and how this distinction resolves the objection that motion cannot be composed of rests because they are opposites.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are not absolute opposites; the direction of motion matters.

The objector argues that motion and rest are opposites, so rest cannot be a component of motion. This would undermine the theory that motion is composed of rests. But the answer reveals a crucial distinction: motion from a space is opposite to rest in that space, but motion to a space is not. Understanding this distinction is key to seeing how motion can indeed be composed of rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the objection and answer.
2
Identify the objection
Locate the opponent's statement and understand why they think motion cannot be composed of rests because of opposition.
The objection is that one opposite cannot be part of another.
3
Unpack the answer's distinction
Notice that the answer distinguishes between 'motion from a space' and 'motion to a space'. Understand why motion to a space is not opposite to rest in that space.
Motion to a space is identical to the first kawm in that space, which is similar to the second kawm (rest).
4
Connect to the theory of motion
Reflect on how this answer salvages the idea that motion is composed of rests, by showing that only one type of motion is opposite to rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ( في إبطال ما التزمه هذا القائل (الحركة) لا شك أنها (ضد السكون فكيف تكون ) الحركة ( مركبة منه فإن أحد الضدين لا يكون جزءاً للآخر (قلنا :) في در هذا الإبطال ليست الحركة والسكون متضادين على الإطلاق بل ( الحركة من الحيز ضد السكون فيه ) إذ لا يتصور اجتماعهما أصلاً ( وأما الحركة إلى الحيز فلا تنافي السكون فيه فإنها أي الحركة إلى الحيز ( نفس الكون ) الأول (فيه ) وذلك لأنَّ الخروج عن الحيز السابق عليه عين الدخول فيه (وهو) أي الكون الأول فيه مماثلاً للكون الثاني فيه ) لما مر من اشتراكهما في أخص صفات النفس ( وأنه ) أي الكون الثاني فيه (سكون ) باتفاق ( فكذا) (هذا أي الكون الأول لأن المتماثلين لا يتخالفان.
For it is said: In refutation of what this speaker accepted: motion is undoubtedly opposite to rest, so how can motion be composed of it? For one opposite cannot be a part of the other. We say: In repelling this refutation, motion and rest are not absolute opposites. Rather, motion from a space is opposite to rest in it, since their coexistence is inconceivable at all. As for motion to a space, it does not conflict with rest in it, because motion to a space is itself the first kawm in it. That is because departure from the preceding space is identical to entry into it. And it (the first kawm in it) is similar to the second kawm in it, due to what has passed of their sharing in the most specific attribute of the self. And that (the second kawm in it) is rest by consensus. So likewise this (the first kawm) is rest, because similar things do not differ.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that motion cannot be composed of rests because motion is the opposite of rest, and one opposite cannot be a part of another. The answer distinguishes between two types of motion: motion from a space and motion to a space. Motion from a space is indeed opposite to rest in that space, as they cannot coexist. However, motion to a space is not opposite to rest in that space; instead, it is identical to the first kawm (occurrence) in that space, which is similar to the second kawm (which is rest). Since the first and second kawms in the same space share the most specific attribute (specifying the substance to that space), they are similar, and since the second is rest by agreement, the first must also be rest. Thus, motion to a space is essentially rest in that space, and only motion from a space is opposite to rest. This nuanced answer shows that the objection only applies to motion from a space, not to motion to a space, and therefore motion as a whole is not composed of rests in a contradictory way.

Key Terms
ضد
سكون
حركة
حيز
كون
تماثل
الخروج
الدخول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion and rest are absolute opposites in all respects.
Correction: Motion and rest are only opposite when considered from the same space. Motion to a space is not opposite to rest in that space; it is actually identical to rest in that space.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute the objection that motion cannot be composed of rests because they are opposites?
A) By denying that motion and rest are opposites
B) By distinguishing motion from a space from motion to a space
C) By stating that motion consists of rests only
D) By claiming that rests are not real opposites

Key Takeaway
Motion can be composed of rests because motion to a space is identical to rest in that space, so the composition does not involve opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that two things in your spiritual life are absolute opposites, like effort and trust, and how does this lesson's distinction between 'motion from' and 'motion to' challenge you to see them as unified? What concrete step will you take this week to turn a 'motion from' into a 'motion to'?
?
If your journey toward Allah can be seen as a series of 'motions to' that are actually 'rests in' His presence, what current struggle or effort can you reinterpret as a moment of rest and closeness to Him? How does that change your attitude toward that struggle starting today?
?
The objector's error was seeing only one aspect of motion. Where in your life are you fixated on a single perspective (e.g., your past mistakes vs. your current state) and missing the nuanced truth that God's mercy encompasses both? Write down one area you will now view with the 'motion to' lens and commit to live it out this week.

Al-Amudi's Objection and the Response: Motion Requires a Second Space
Al-Amudi objects that similarity does not imply motion, and the response that motion requires a second kawm in another space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amudi's objection that similarity does not imply motion and the author's response that motion requires a second kawm in a second space.

Core Idea
Motion is not mere similarity but requires a second kawm in a second space.

Al-Amudi challenges the idea that sharing attributes implies similarity, and pushes an absurd conclusion. The author's response clarifies that motion is defined by a relational condition involving two spaces, not just similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Take note of al-Amudi's objection and the author's answer.
2
Identify al-Amudi's objections
Al-Amudi gives two points: (1) sharing some attributes does not prove similarity, and (2) if similarity held, the second kawm would be motion, which is absurd.
3
Understand the author's response
The author indicates that motion requires considering a second kawm in a second space. What is true of the first kawm (motion) is not true of a single kawm in the same space.
The key phrase is 'except that he considers in motion a single locus'.
4
Recognize the part-whole distinction
The explanation distinguishes between what is attributed to the part (a single kawm) and what is attributed to the whole (a motion consisting of two kawms).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الآمدي : ذلك الاشتراك لا يوجب التماثل لأن المتخالفين قد يشتركان في بعض الصفات ولا نسلم أنَّ ما ذكره أخص صفاتهما (و) أيضاً (يلزمهم أن يكون الكون الثاني حركة لأنه مثل الكون الأول وهو (حركة باتفاق، وكذا الثاني قال الآمدي : وهذا إشكال مشكل ولعل عند غيري جوابه وأشار المصنف إلى الجواب بقوله إلا أن يعتبر أي يلزمهم أن يكون الكون الثاني حركة، إلا أن يعتبر ( في الحركة على محل واحد ضرورة أنَّ ما يحمل عليه الجزء حينئذ غير ما يحمل عليه الكل.
Al-Amudi said: That sharing does not necessitate similarity, because two different things may share in some attributes, and we do not grant that what he mentioned is the most specific of their attributes. And also, it follows for them that the second kawm would be motion, because it is like the first kawm, and it is motion by agreement. Likewise the second. Al-Amudi said: This is a difficult problem, and perhaps someone else has its answer. The author indicated the answer by saying: 'Except that he considers in motion' – meaning it follows for them that the second kawm is motion, except that he considers in motion a single locus, because what is attributed to the part then is not what is attributed to the whole.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Amudi and the author's response. Al-Amudi first objects that mere sharing of some attributes does not prove similarity between the first and second kawms in a space, because two different things can share some attributes. He further argues that if we accept the similarity, then the same reasoning would force the conclusion that the second kawm (which is agreed to be rest) is actually motion, because it is similar to the first kawm (which is motion by agreement). This would be absurd. Al-Amudi admits this is a difficult problem and hopes someone else has an answer. The author (al-Iji) then indicates the answer by saying 'except that he considers in motion' – meaning that motion is defined not just by similarity but by considering a second kawm in a second space as a necessary condition. The explanation is that what is attributed to the part (the first kawm in a space) is not the same as what is attributed to the whole (the motion comprising two kawms in two spaces). Thus, the second kawm in the same space is not motion because it lacks the relation to a second space. This preserves the distinction that motion requires two successive kawms in different spaces, while rest is a single kawm in one space.

Key Terms
تماثل
اشتراك
كون
حركة
سكون
حيز
جزء
كل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity in one attribute implies identity, so the second kawm must be motion.
Correction: Motion requires not just similarity but a second kawm in a different space. The second kawm in the same space lacks this condition.

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for something to be motion according to the author's answer?
A) It must be similar to rest
B) It must involve a second kawm in a different space
C) It must be composed of rests
D) It must be opposite to rest

Key Takeaway
Motion requires a second kawm in a second space, not just similarity to the first kawm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because two things share some qualities, they are the same? How might this mistake affect your judgment of people or your understanding of Allah's actions? Write down one area where you need to pause.
?
The objection shows that a hasty conclusion can lead to absurdity. Where in your own thinking about faith or daily life have you drawn conclusions without considering the necessary conditions? Make a commitment to be more precise.
?
How does paying careful attention to definitions (like motion requiring a second space) protect you from errors in your beliefs? What is one belief you hold that you have not examined with such precision? Take action to investigate it.

Motion from a Locus: Definition and the Claim of Similarity
Defines motion and introduces the similarity claim between first and second atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn define motion from a locus and why the first atom in a new locus is claimed to be similar to rest.

Core Idea
Motion from a locus is opposite to rest, but motion to a locus is identical with rest in that locus.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in kalām physics: between motion from a locus and motion to a locus. The author argues that motion to a locus is actually the same as the first atom in that locus, which is claimed to be similar to the second atom (rest). This sets up a puzzle that will be addressed later.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author distinguishes between 'motion from a locus' and 'motion to a locus'.
The key term 'ḥayyiz' (locus) is central.
2
Understand the similarity claim
The author claims that the first atom in a locus is similar to the second atom (rest) because they share the most specific attribute of the self. This is a critical point for the argument.
The concept of 'tamāthul' (similarity) is used to link motion and rest.
3
Reflect on the puzzle
Ask yourself: if the first atom is similar to rest, then why is it considered motion? This tension will be addressed in the next lesson.
This prepares you for al-Āmidī's objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة من الحيز ضد السكون فيه إذ لا يتصور اجتماعهما أصلا وأما الحركة إلى الحيز فلا تنافي السكون فيه فإنها أي الحركة إلى الحيز نفس الكون الأول فيه وذلك لأن الخروج عن الحيز السابق عليه عين الدخول فيه وهو أي الكون الأول فيه مماثلا للكون الثاني فيه لما مر من اشتراكهما في أخص صفات النفس وأنه أي الكون الثاني فيه سكون باتفاق فكذا هذا أي الكون الأول لأن المتماثلين لا يتخالفان قال الآمدي ذلك الاشتراك لا يوجب التماثل لأن المتخالفين قد يشتركان في بعض الصفات ولا نسلم أن ما ذكره أخص صفاتهما وأيضا يلزمهم أن يكون الكون الثاني حركة لأنه مثل الكون الأول وهو حركة باتفاق وكذا الثاني قال الآمدي وهذا إشكال مشكل ولعل عند غيري جوابه وأشار المصنف إلى الجواب بقوله إلا أن يعتبر في الحركة أن تكون مسبوقة بالحصول في ذلك الحيز إلا أن تكون مسبوقة بالحصول في حيز آخر كما مر إذ على هذا لا يكون الكون الثاني حركة لأنه مسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز ويكون الكون الأول حركة عن المكان السابق مع كونه سكونا في هذا المكان والحاصل أنا لا نعتبر في السكون المسبوقية بكون آخر حتى يكون الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكونا ونلتزم حينئذ أن تكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات لكنا نعتبر في الحركة عدم المسبوقية بالكون في ذلك الحيز ولا نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة بالكون في حيز آخر حتى لا يلزمنا أن يكون الكون الثاني حركة وإنما حملنا عبارة الكتاب على اعتبار الأمرين معا في الحركة إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط كما هو ظاهرها لزم أن يكون الكون في أول زمان الحدوث حركة ولا قائل به ثم أورد على جوابه إشكالا بقوله وحينئذ أي حين اعتبر في الحركة ما ذكر اندفع ذلك الإشكال لكن لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات لأن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءا للحركة وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون أن جميع أجزاء الحركة سكنات لأن كل سكون حركة فلكون الأول في المكان الثاني من حيث إيجابه لاختصاصه بالمكان الثاني مماثل للكون الثاني فيه فيكون سكونا وليس مماثلا من حيث الخروج من الحيز الأول فلا يكون الكون الثاني حركة فاندفع الإشكال الثاني وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح بقوله ويكون الكون الأول المكان السابق وبما حررنا ظهر أن ما قيل أن الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين وبأن الكون الثاني سكون كما يستلزم كون الأول سكونا كذلك كون الأول حركة فلا يتجه الجواب المذكور ليس بشيء كما لا يخفى قوله حتى يكون الكون إلخ غاية للنفي لا للمنفي قوله ولا نكتفي إلخ نعتبر في الحركة مجموع الأمرين ونقول الحركة الكون الأول في المكان الثاني والسكون الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والكون الأول في المكان الأول قوله حتى لا يلزمنا إلخ غاية لقوله نعتبر في الحركة لا لقوله ولا نكتفي كما توهم فإنه فائدة ما ذكره بقوله إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط قوله وهو مردود إلخ قيل الظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة سكنات لزوم كون الحركة مجموع سكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنيا على قولهم بتماثل الحصولين مجموع الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكونا يستلزم كون الأول كذلك فلا يتجه حينئذ الجواب المذكور قوله ولا نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة إلخ فيه بحث إذ قد سبق أن مرادهم بالسبق السبق بالاتصال فيكتفي بما مر فيعدم لزوم كون الكون الثاني حركة قوله وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون إلخ فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد لو كان وجه قول
Motion from a locus is opposite to rest in it, for their coexistence is not conceivable at all. As for motion to a locus, it does not contradict rest in it, for it—i.e., motion to a locus—is the same as the first atom (al-kawn al-awwal) in it. That is because exiting the previous locus is exactly entering it. And it—i.e., the first atom in it—is similar (mumāthil) to the second atom in it, due to what has passed of their sharing the most specific attributes of the self (an-nafs). And that it—i.e., the second atom in it—is rest (sukūn) by agreement. So likewise this—i.e., the first atom—because similars do not differ. Al-Āmidī said: That shared attribute does not necessitate similarity, for different things may share some attributes, and we do not concede that what he mentioned is their most specific attribute. Also, it would entail for them that the second atom is motion, because it is similar to the first atom, and it—i.e., the first atom—is motion by agreement, and likewise the second. Al-Āmidī said: This is a problematic difficulty, and perhaps someone else has an answer for it. The author pointed to the answer by saying: Unless one considers in motion that it be preceded by occurrence in that locus, unless it be preceded by occurrence in another locus, as has passed. For on this basis, the second atom would not be motion because it is preceded by another atom in that locus, and the first atom would be motion from the previous place while being rest in this place. The conclusion is that we do not consider in rest being preceded by another atom, so that the atom at the first moment of occurrence would be rest, and we then commit that motion is composed of rests (sakanāt). But we consider in motion the lack of being preceded by an atom in that locus, and we are not content with what has passed of its being preceded by an atom in another locus, so that we are not forced to say that the second atom is motion. We interpret the text of the book as considering both aspects together in motion, for if it were interpreted as considering only the first aspect, as its apparent meaning suggests, then the atom at the first moment of occurrence would be motion, and no one says that. Then he raised an objection to his answer by saying: Then—i.e., when the aforementioned is considered in motion—that difficulty is repelled, but motion would not be a sum of rests, because the second atom is rest and not a part of motion. This is refuted by their claim that all parts of motion are rests, because every rest is motion. So the first atom in the second place, in terms of its necessitating its occupation of the second place, is similar to the second atom in it, so it would be rest, and it is not similar in terms of exiting the first locus, so the second atom would not be motion. Thus the second difficulty is repelled. The commentator pointed to what we have mentioned by saying: 'And the first atom is [motion] from the previous place...' From what we have set out, it is clear that the claim that the discussion is polemical (ilzāmī) against one who holds the similarity of the two occurrences, and that if the second atom is rest it entails that the first is also rest, just as the first being motion [would entail the second being motion], so the mentioned answer does not apply, is nothing, as is not hidden. His statement: 'So that the atom be...' is a limit (ghāya) for the negation, not for the negated. His statement: 'We are not content...' We consider in motion the combination of the two aspects and say: motion is the first atom in the second place, and rest is the second atom in the second place and the first atom in the first place. His statement: 'So that it does not entail for us...' is a limit for his statement 'We consider in motion', not for his statement 'We are not content', as might be imagined, for it is a benefit of what he mentioned in his statement: 'For if it were interpreted as considering only the first aspect.' His statement: 'It is refuted...' It is said: The apparent meaning of his statement 'Then motion would not be rests' is the necessity that motion be a sum of rests, and their commitment to it was based on their statement of the similarity of the two occurrences. The sum of the discourse is polemical against one who holds the similarity of the two occurrences and that the second being rest entails the first being likewise, so then the mentioned answer would not apply. His statement: 'We are not content with what has passed of its being preceded...' There is investigation in it, for it has already passed that their intent by 'precedence' is precedence by contiguity (ittiṣāl), so what has passed suffices, and the entailment that the second atom is motion is absent. His statement: 'It is refuted by their claim...' There is investigation in it, for this only refutes if the aspect of the saying was...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argumentative discussion about motion (ḥaraka) and rest (sukūn) as understood by the mutakallimūn. The author begins by defining motion from a locus (al-ḥaraka min al-ḥayyiz) as the opposite of rest in that locus, stating that they cannot coexist. He then distinguishes motion towards a locus (al-ḥaraka ilā al-ḥayyiz), explaining that it is not opposite to rest in that locus because it is identical with the first atom (al-kawn al-awwal) in that locus: the act of exiting a previous locus is exactly the same as entering the new one. This first atom in the new locus is said to be similar (mumāthil) to the second atom in that locus (which is rest by agreement) because they share the most specific attribute of the self (khāṣṣu ṣifāt an-nafs). Therefore, by the rule that similars do not differ, the first atom should also be rest, not motion. Al-Āmidī objects that the shared attribute does not necessitate similarity, because different things can share attributes, and he does not concede that the mentioned attribute is the most specific. Then there is a second objection: if the first atom is motion and the second is rest, then by similarity the second should be motion, leading to a contradiction. Al-Āmidī calls this a difficult problem. The author's answer is given: motion must be considered as being preceded by occurrence in another locus (not the same one), so that the first atom (motion) is preceded by occurrence in the previous locus, while the second atom (rest) is preceded by another atom in the same locus. Thus, the second atom is not motion. The author also clarifies that they do not require rest to be preceded by another atom, so the first atom at the moment of occurrence is rest in that locus, and motion is composed of rests (sakanāt). They consider two aspects for motion: being the first atom in the second place (rest) and being preceded by occurrence in another place (departure). The objection that this makes motion not a sum of rests is refuted by the claim that all parts of motion are rests, as every rest is motion from a certain perspective. The passage ends by addressing various subsidiary points, emphasizing that the discussion is not merely polemical but based on the specific conditions of similarity and priority.

Key Terms
حركة (ḥaraka)
سكون (sukūn)
كون (kawn)
حيز (ḥayyiz)
تماثل (tamāthul)
أخص صفات النفس (akhaṣṣ ṣifāt an-nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are simple opposites without any nuance.
Correction: The passage shows that motion towards a locus is actually identical with the first atom in that locus and is claimed similar to rest, indicating a more complex relationship.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is motion towards a locus not opposite to rest in that locus?
A) Because they can coexist
B) Because it is the same as the first atom in that locus
C) Because the author denies similarity
D) Because rest is not an atom

Key Takeaway
Motion to a locus is identical with the first atom in that locus and is claimed to be similar to rest, producing a puzzle about its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you move toward a good deed, is your first moment truly a step forward or merely a rest in familiar routine? How can you ensure your initial intention is not just similar to rest but genuinely transformative?
?
The passage reveals that motion to a locus is identical with the first atom in that locus. Reflect on your spiritual movements: Are you aware that each new beginning is composed of discrete moments? What accountability does that place on you to make each 'first atom' count for eternity?
?
If similars cannot differ, then your actions that resemble rest may actually be rest. Examine your life: Which of your 'pious movements' might be merely static—outwardly similar to devotion but lacking true motion toward Allah? What will you change today to break that similarity?

Rational Judgments

Al-Āmidī's Objections and the Author's Answer
Presents al-Āmidī's objections and the author's answer involving precedence and composition from rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's objections to the similarity argument and the author's refined definition of motion involving precedence and composition from rests.

Core Idea
Al-Āmidī challenges similarity; the author answers by requiring motion to be preceded by another locus.

This lesson continues the discussion with al-Āmidī's critical objections. He argues that shared attributes do not guarantee similarity and that the argument leads to contradictions. The author then provides a nuanced answer that distinguishes motion and rest by considering precedence in occurrence, and even argues that motion is composed of rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read al-Āmidī's two objections carefully: first, that shared attributes don't necessitate similarity; second, that if the first atom is motion, the second should be motion too.
Note the phrase 'إشكال مشكل' – a problematic difficulty.
2
Study the author's answer
The author answers by defining motion as an atom that is not preceded by another atom in the same locus, but is preceded by an atom in a different locus. This distinguishes it from rest, which is not preceded.
Pay attention to 'مسبوقية' (precedence) and 'اتصال' (contiguity).
3
Understand the composition from rests
The author accepts that motion is composed of rests (sakanāt) because each atom in a new locus is a rest, and the exiting motion is the entire sequence. This refutes the objection that motion would not be a sum of rests.
This is a key commitment of the mutakallimūn.
4
Review the refutation of the counter-objection
The passage ends by addressing a criticism that the discussion is merely polemical. The author insists that the answer stands on its own based on the conditions of similarity and precedence.
This shows the robustness of the argument.

الكتاب على اعتبار الأمرين معا في الحركة إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط كما هو ظاهرها لزم أن يكون الكون في أول زمان الحدوث حركة ولا قائل به ثم أورد على جوابه إشكالا بقوله وحينئذ أي حين اعتبر في الحركة ما ذكر اندفع ذلك الإشكال لكن لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات لأن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءا للحركة وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون أن جميع أجزاء الحركة سكنات لأن كل سكون حركة فلكون الأول في المكان الثاني من حيث إيجابه لاختصاصه بالمكان الثاني مماثل للكون الثاني فيه فيكون سكونا وليس مماثلا من حيث الخروج من الحيز الأول فلا يكون الكون الثاني حركة فاندفع الإشكال الثاني وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح بقوله ويكون الكون الأول المكان السابق وبما حررنا ظهر أن ما قيل أن الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين وبأن الكون الثاني سكون كما يستلزم كون الأول سكونا كذلك كون الأول حركة فلا يتجه الجواب المذكور ليس بشيء كما لا يخفى قوله حتى يكون الكون إلخ غاية للنفي لا للمنفي قوله ولا نكتفي إلخ نعتبر في الحركة مجموع الأمرين ونقول الحركة الكون الأول في المكان الثاني والسكون الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني والكون الأول في المكان الأول قوله حتى لا يلزمنا إلخ غاية لقوله نعتبر في الحركة لا لقوله ولا نكتفي كما توهم فإنه فائدة ما ذكره بقوله إذ لو حملت على اعتبار الأول فقط قوله وهو مردود إلخ قيل الظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة سكنات لزوم كون الحركة مجموع سكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنيا على قولهم بتماثل الحصولين مجموع الكلام إلزامي لمن يقول بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكونا يستلزم كون الأول كذلك فلا يتجه حينئذ الجواب المذكور قوله ولا نكتفي بما مر من كونها مسبوقة إلخ فيه بحث إذ قد سبق أن مرادهم بالسبق السبق بالاتصال فيكتفي بما مر فيعدم لزوم كون الكون الثاني حركة قوله وهو مردود بأنهم يدعون إلخ فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد لو كان وجه قول
Motion from a locus is opposite to rest in it, for their coexistence is not conceivable at all. As for motion to a locus, it does not contradict rest in it, for it—i.e., motion to a locus—is the same as the first atom (al-kawn al-awwal) in it. That is because exiting the previous locus is exactly entering it. And it—i.e., the first atom in it—is similar (mumāthil) to the second atom in it, due to what has passed of their sharing the most specific attributes of the self (an-nafs). And that it—i.e., the second atom in it—is rest (sukūn) by agreement. So likewise this—i.e., the first atom—because similars do not differ. Al-Āmidī said: That shared attribute does not necessitate similarity, for different things may share some attributes, and we do not concede that what he mentioned is their most specific attribute. Also, it would entail for them that the second atom is motion, because it is similar to the first atom, and it—i.e., the first atom—is motion by agreement, and likewise the second. Al-Āmidī said: This is a problematic difficulty, and perhaps someone else has an answer for it. The author pointed to the answer by saying: Unless one considers in motion that it be preceded by occurrence in that locus, unless it be preceded by occurrence in another locus, as has passed. For on this basis, the second atom would not be motion because it is preceded by another atom in that locus, and the first atom would be motion from the previous place while being rest in this place. The conclusion is that we do not consider in rest being preceded by another atom, so that the atom at the first moment of occurrence would be rest, and we then commit that motion is composed of rests (sakanāt). But we consider in motion the lack of being preceded by an atom in that locus, and we are not content with what has passed of its being preceded by an atom in another locus, so that we are not forced to say that the second atom is motion. We interpret the text of the book as considering both aspects together in motion, for if it were interpreted as considering only the first aspect, as its apparent meaning suggests, then the atom at the first moment of occurrence would be motion, and no one says that. Then he raised an objection to his answer by saying: Then—i.e., when the aforementioned is considered in motion—that difficulty is repelled, but motion would not be a sum of rests, because the second atom is rest and not a part of motion. This is refuted by their claim that all parts of motion are rests, because every rest is motion. So the first atom in the second place, in terms of its necessitating its occupation of the second place, is similar to the second atom in it, so it would be rest, and it is not similar in terms of exiting the first locus, so the second atom would not be motion. Thus the second difficulty is repelled. The commentator pointed to what we have mentioned by saying: 'And the first atom is [motion] from the previous place...' From what we have set out, it is clear that the claim that the discussion is polemical (ilzāmī) against one who holds the similarity of the two occurrences, and that if the second atom is rest it entails that the first is also rest, just as the first being motion [would entail the second being motion], so the mentioned answer does not apply, is nothing, as is not hidden. His statement: 'So that the atom be...' is a limit (ghāya) for the negation, not for the negated. His statement: 'We are not content...' We consider in motion the combination of the two aspects and say: motion is the first atom in the second place, and rest is the second atom in the second place and the first atom in the first place. His statement: 'So that it does not entail for us...' is a limit for his statement 'We consider in motion', not for his statement 'We are not content', as might be imagined, for it is a benefit of what he mentioned in his statement: 'For if it were interpreted as considering only the first aspect.' His statement: 'It is refuted...' It is said: The apparent meaning of his statement 'Then motion would not be rests' is the necessity that motion be a sum of rests, and their commitment to it was based on their statement of the similarity of the two occurrences. The sum of the discourse is polemical against one who holds the similarity of the two occurrences and that the second being rest entails the first being likewise, so then the mentioned answer would not apply. His statement: 'We are not content with what has passed of its being preceded...' There is investigation in it, for it has already passed that their intent by 'precedence' is precedence by contiguity (ittiṣāl), so what has passed suffices, and the entailment that the second atom is motion is absent. His statement: 'It is refuted by their claim...' There is investigation in it, for this only refutes if the aspect of the saying was...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argumentative discussion about motion (ḥaraka) and rest (sukūn) as understood by the mutakallimūn. The author begins by defining motion from a locus (al-ḥaraka min al-ḥayyiz) as the opposite of rest in that locus, stating that they cannot coexist. He then distinguishes motion towards a locus (al-ḥaraka ilā al-ḥayyiz), explaining that it is not opposite to rest in that locus because it is identical with the first atom (al-kawn al-awwal) in that locus: the act of exiting a previous locus is exactly the same as entering the new one. This first atom in the new locus is said to be similar (mumāthil) to the second atom in that locus (which is rest by agreement) because they share the most specific attribute of the self (khāṣṣu ṣifāt an-nafs). Therefore, by the rule that similars do not differ, the first atom should also be rest, not motion. Al-Āmidī objects that the shared attribute does not necessitate similarity, because different things can share attributes, and he does not concede that the mentioned attribute is the most specific. Then there is a second objection: if the first atom is motion and the second is rest, then by similarity the second should be motion, leading to a contradiction. Al-Āmidī calls this a difficult problem. The author's answer is given: motion must be considered as being preceded by occurrence in another locus (not the same one), so that the first atom (motion) is preceded by occurrence in the previous locus, while the second atom (rest) is preceded by another atom in the same locus. Thus, the second atom is not motion. The author also clarifies that they do not require rest to be preceded by another atom, so the first atom at the moment of occurrence is rest in that locus, and motion is composed of rests (sakanāt). They consider two aspects for motion: being the first atom in the second place (rest) and being preceded by occurrence in another place (departure). The objection that this makes motion not a sum of rests is refuted by the claim that all parts of motion are rests, as every rest is motion from a certain perspective. The passage ends by addressing various subsidiary points, emphasizing that the discussion is not merely polemical but based on the specific conditions of similarity and priority.

Key Terms
تماثل (tamāthul)
مسبوقية (masbūqiyya)
اتصال (ittiṣāl)
سكنات (sakanāt)
إشكال (ishkāl)
جواب (jawāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Āmidī's objections are unanswerable.
Correction: The author provides a coherent answer by adding the condition of precedence, showing the objections can be resolved.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, what condition distinguishes motion from rest?
A) Motion is not preceded by any atom
B) Motion is preceded by an atom in the same locus
C) Motion is preceded by an atom in a different locus
D) Rest is preceded by an atom in a different locus

Key Takeaway
Al-Āmidī's objections are answered by defining motion through temporal precedence in another locus, leading to the view that motion is composed of rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult objection to your faith, like al-Āmidī's challenge, do you tend to dismiss it or dig deeper for an answer? Where is the line between intellectual humility and laziness in your own heart?
?
The author insists that motion is composed of rests, meaning change is built from moments of stillness. In your spiritual life, where are you mistaking busyness for growth, and neglecting the 'rests' that might actually compose real progress?
?
Al-Āmidī presented his objection as a 'problematic difficulty' and hoped others would answer it. Are you more likely to be the one who raises doubts or the one who seeks to resolve them for the sake of truth? What would it cost you to switch roles?

Motion and Terminological Disputes
Refutes an objection by defining motion types and calling the dispute terminological.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection about a body moving in two opposite directions simultaneously is dismissed as a terminological dispute rather than a real contradiction.

Core Idea
Not every disagreement is doctrinal; some are just over words.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection in kalam: how can a single body move in two opposite directions at once? The author quickly shows that this is only impossible for one type of motion, not all. He then reveals that the entire controversy boils down to how one defines the word 'motion'—a terminological dispute, not a genuine theological conflict. The commentary further clarifies that the dispute is about the application of the term, not its technical coinage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that a single moving body cannot move in two directions simultaneously. Read the passage to see how the author first refutes this by dividing motion into two types.
The division is key: motion that displaces the body vs. motion that displaces its place.
2
See how the division defuses the objection
The author states that the impossible case only applies to the first type of motion, not the second. The example given by the objector falls under the second type, so the objection fails.
3
Recognize the terminological nature of the dispute
The author points out that al-Ustadh defined motion broadly, so he naturally admits two motions in two directions. Opponents define motion narrowly. The disagreement is purely about naming.
The passage also mentions a parallel dispute about 'rest' to illustrate the pattern.
4
Grasp the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that al-Ustadh was not inventing a new technical term but investigating the real essence of motion. Thus the dispute is about the application of the word, not its coinage.
This addresses a criticism from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باطل بالضرورة ) فيقال : ( لدفع هذا الإلزام : الحركة قسمان قسم يزول به المتحرك عن مكانه، وقسم لا يزول به عنه بل يزول به مكانه عنه ( وذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه من كون الشيء الواحد في حالة واحدة متحركاً إلى جهتين ( إنما يمتنع في حركة يزول بها المتحرك عن مكانه دون ما يزول بها المكان عنه كما في الصورة التي فرضتموها ( وشدد النكير عليه ) أي على قول الأستاذ ) ولا معنى له ) أي للإنكار وتشديده (لأنه نزاع في التسمية) فإنّ الأستاذ أطلق اسم الحركة على اختلاف المحاذيات سواء كان مبدأ الاختلاف في المتحرك أو في غيره فلزمه اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين، فالتزمه كما أن جماعة أطلقوا اسم السكون على الكون مطلقاً، فلزمهم تركب الحركة من السكنات بل كون الحركة عن المكان الأول عين السكون في المكان الثاني فالتزموهما، والمخالفون له يطلقونه على القسم الأول ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاحات .
الحركة بإزائها هي لفظ من قوله : (اطلق اسم الحركة إلخ لا أنه اصطلح على ذلك بل لأن الماهية التي وضع تبدل المحاذاة سواء كان مبدأ المتبدل فيه أو في غيره فلا يرد ما في شرح المقاصد من أن كونه نزاعاً في التسمية، ليس على ما بنبغي لأن ماذكره الأستاذ في بيان الحيز أو الحركة أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحاً منهم على أن نجعله اسماً لذلك، وإلا لما كان لجعله المسائل الكلامية والاستدلال عليه بالدلالة العقلية معنى تحقيقاً للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز والحركة وما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائها وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح بقوله : أطلق أي نسبة التسمية في المتن بمعنى الإطلاق لا بمعنى الوضع فهذا نزاع في إطلاق اللفظ، وأن المعنى الذي يطلق لفظ الحركة في جميع اللغات ما هو وليس نزاعاً راجعاً إلى الوضع والاصطلاح.
قوله : ( لأنه نزاع في التسمية قال في شرح المقاصد : وما ذكره في المواقف من أن هذا نزاع في التسمية ليس على ما ينبغي، لأن ما ذكره الأستاذ وغيره في بيان الحيز والحركة أنه هذا أو ذاك ليس اصطلاحاً ،منهم على أنا نجعله اسماً لذلك الحيز وإلا لما كان لجعله من المسائل العلمية أو الاستدلال عليه بالأدلة العقلية معنى بل تحقيق للماهية التي وضع لفظ الحيز والحركة أو ما يرادفه من جميع اللغات بإزائهم وإثبات ذاتياتها بعد تصورها بالحقيقة حين الحكم بأن هذا في حيز وذلك في آخر وأن هذا متحرك وذاك ساكن.
('False necessarily' - so it is said: to repel this objection: motion is of two types: a type by which the moving thing departs from its place, and a type by which it does not depart from it but its place departs from it. And that which you mentioned - of one thing in one state moving towards two directions - is only impossible in motion by which the moving thing departs from its place, not that by which the place departs from it, as in the picture you assumed. And he severely rebuked it - i.e., the statement of the master - and it has no meaning - i.e., the rebuke and its severity - because it is a dispute over naming. For the master applied the name 'motion' to the variation of oppositions, whether the origin of the variation is in the moving thing or in something else, so he was required to allow two motions toward two directions, so he accepted it, just as a group applied the name 'rest' to existence absolutely, so they were required to compound motion from rests, rather that motion from the first place is the very rest in the second place, so they accepted them. And those who oppose him apply it to the first type, and there is no quarreling over terminologies.)
(Motion is what is placed in front of it) - a word from his statement: 'He applied the name motion, etc.' - not that he stipulated it, but because the essence for which the word is placed is the change of alignment, whether the origin of what changes is in it or in something else. So what is in Sharh al-Maqasid - that it is a dispute over naming - is not as it should be, because what the master and others mentioned in explaining 'place' or 'motion' that it is this or that is not a stipulation from them that we make it a name for that, otherwise there would be no point in making it one of the theological matters and reasoning about it with rational evidence, but rather a verification of the essence for which the word 'place' and 'motion' and their equivalents in all languages are placed. And to what we mentioned the commentator pointed by his saying: 'applied' - i.e., the attribution of naming in the text in the sense of application, not in the sense of coining, so this is a dispute over the application of the word, and what is the meaning for which the word 'motion' is applied in all languages, and it is not a dispute referring to coining and stipulation.)
His statement: ('Because it is a dispute over naming') - He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: What was mentioned in al-Mawaqif - that this is a dispute over naming - is not as it should be, because what the master and others mentioned in explaining 'place' and 'motion' that it is this or that is not a stipulation from them that we make it a name for that place, otherwise there would be no point in making it one of the scientific matters or reasoning about it with rational evidence, but rather a verification of the essence for which the word 'place' and 'motion' or their equivalents in all languages are placed, and the establishment of its essential attributes after conceiving it in reality when judging that this is in a place and that is in another, and that this is moving and that is stationary.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the impossibility of a single object moving in two opposite directions simultaneously. The author first divides motion into two types: one where the mover leaves its place, and another where the place leaves the mover (as in circular motion). He then states that the objector's assumed scenario is only impossible in the first type, not the second, which is like the example they gave. The author then notes that the master (al-Ustadh) applied the term 'motion' to any change of alignment, regardless of cause, thus accepting two motions to two directions. Some others applied 'rest' to existence itself, requiring motion to be composed of rests. The dispute is merely about terminology. The commentator adds that this is not a stipulation but a verification of the essence of motion, so it is not a mere terminological dispute as some claimed. The passage concludes that the disagreement is about the application of the word 'motion' across languages, not about its technical coinage.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
نزاع في التسمية
إطلاق اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about two motions in two directions reveals a genuine contradiction in the master's view.
Correction: The master's view is consistent because he defines motion broadly; the objection only holds under a narrower definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the objection about two motions to two directions is invalid?
A) Because motion is only one type.
B) Because the case assumed is only impossible in motion where the mover leaves its place, not where the place leaves the mover.
C) Because the objector misunderstood the nature of space.
D) Because the master accepted motion to two directions.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a body cannot move in two directions at once is resolved by distinguishing types of motion and recognizing the dispute as merely terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you dismissed someone's argument as false without first checking whether you both meant the same thing by the key words? How might that have changed the outcome?
?
The master was willing to accept the apparent absurdity of two motions in one thing because he cared more about the true meaning of 'motion' than about comfort. Is there a belief you hold simply because it feels familiar, even if you haven't examined whether the words you use match reality?
?
If you knew that many of your disagreements with others are purely terminological, would you still hold onto them with the same intensity? What would it cost you to let go of a word and focus on the meaning behind it?

The Contact of a Single Atom with Multiple Atoms
States the agreement on a single atom surrounded by six atoms and refutes those who limit contact to one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological position that a single atom can contact multiple atoms without requiring division, and the refutation of the opposing view.

Core Idea
A single atom can contact multiple atoms without dividing.

This passage from a classical kalam text addresses a fundamental question about the composition of bodies from atoms. The author states the consensus position that a single atom can be surrounded by six contacting atoms, and then refutes the minority view that limited contact to one atom. The discussion reveals the subtle reasoning used to defend the possibility of multiple contacts without requiring the atom's division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms like 'جوهر فرد' and 'ملاقاة'.
The passage combines the main text (matn) with commentary (sharh). Focus on the argument first.
2
Identify the consensus and the exception
Find the statement of agreement among theologians (اتفق القائلون) and the exception of some who denied contact with more than one atom.
The exception is a specific group of theologians.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
The objection was fearing division (تجزئة) of the atom. The refutation uses sense-perception and the logical necessity of body composition.
Note that the author calls the objection 'stubborn denial' (مكابرة).
4
Examine the analogy of the point
The commentary introduces an analogy of a center point aligning with multiple points on a circumference, showing multiple relations without division.
This is a key subtle point (دقيق) that resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفق القائلون بالأكوان على أنه ( يجوز وجود جوهر فرد محفوف بستة جواهر ملاقية له ( من جهاته الست إلا ما نقل عن بعض المتكلمين) من ( أنه منع ذلك )
ولم يجوز ملاقاة الجوهر الفرد لأكثر من جوهر واحد ( حذراً من لزوم تجزئه وهو مكابرة وإنكار (للمحسوس) فإنَّ الحس يشهد بالتلاقي بين الجواهر من جميع الجهات (و) هو (مانع من تأليف الأجسام من الجواهر الفردة، فإنه إذا لم يمكن التلاقي من جميع الجوانب كيف يتحصل منها الجسم الطويل العريض العميق، بل لا يكون هناك إلا جواهر مبثوثة غير متلاقية ولا ممكنة التلاقي ( واتفقوا ) أيضاً (على المجاورة والتأليف بين ذلك الجوهر والجواهر المحيطة به، ثم اختلفوا فقال الشيخ الأشعري : ( والمعتزلة المجاورة ) أي الاجتماع الذي هو كون الجوهرين بحيث لا
قوله : ( حذراً من لزوم إلخ) فإنه إذ لاقى بجوهر واحد كان هناك ملاقاة واحدة قائمة بذلك الجوهر لا ببعضه فلا يلزم انقسامه والتداخل لعدم الاتحاد في الحيز بخلاف ما إذا لاقى بجوهرين فإن ملاقاته لآخر فيتعدد محل الملاقاتين فيلزم انقسام الجوهر لكن للجمهور أن يمنعوا استلزام تعدد الملاقاة بتعدد المحل لم لا يجوز أن تكون الملاقاة متعددة بحسب ما يلاقي له قائمة بنفس الجوهر من غير تعدد فيه كمحاذاة نقطة المركز للنقاط المفروضة في المحيط فإنها متعددة تلك النقاط بالمركز من غير لزوم انقسام المركز والقول بأن الملاقاة إن كان بالبعض يلزم الإنقسام وإن كان بالكل يلزم التداخل حكم وهمي ناشيء من قياس ملاقاة من غير المنقسم على ملاقاة المنقسم فتدبر فإنه دقيق.
قوله : ( وإنكار) إشارة إلى أن تعدية المكابرة باللام بتضمين معنى الإنكار .
قوله : ( فإن الحس إلخ) أي العقل بعد ثبوت الجوهر الفرد بمعونة الحس بالتلاقي وإنما قلنا : إنّ الحاكم هو العقل بناء على أن التلاقي ليس من المحسوسات بالذات .
قوله : (وهو) مانع أي عدم التلاقي المفهوم من قوله : منع ذلك مانع عن تأليف الأجسام.
قوله : (كيف يتحصل إلخ) إن أراد عدم تحصل الطول والعرض والعمق في نفس الأمر فمسلم لكن على القول بالملاقاة أيضاً يلزم ذلك بوجود المفاصل بين الجواهر وإن كانت متلاقية، ولذا أنكر المتكلمون المقدار وإن أراد عدم التحصل في الحس فممنوع، فاته إذا كانت مبثوثة لا يحصل التأليف لأنه يقتضي استلزام حركة الأخر، ففيه أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لإرادة الفاعل المختار من غير ملاقاة بينهما .
قوله : ( حذرا من لزوم تجزئه) وقد يقال : التجزو لازم على تقدير ملاقاة جوهر واحد لمثله إذ ملاقاة كل منهما للآخر ببعضه لا كله، لأنه إن انطبق أحدهما على الآخر بحيث اتحدا وضعاً لم يحصل منهما جسم ذو حجم إلا أن لزوم التجزؤ على الأول ظاهر فلذا منعه ذلك البعض والتزم الثاني .
قوله : ( فإن الحس يشهد إلخ) أي الحدس الحسي بواسطة إحساس التلاقي بين الجواهر من الجهات يشهد بذلك إلا أنه أحس بملاقاة جوهر فرد لجواهر متعددة من جميع جميع وهذا ظاهر.
Those who affirm accidents (al-akwan) agreed that (the existence of a single atom surrounded by six atoms touching it from its six directions is possible, except what is transmitted from some theologians) that (they denied that).
And they did not allow the contact of a single atom with more than one atom, (out of fear of requiring its division, and it is a stubborn denial of the sensible, for sense bears witness to contact between atoms from all directions. And it is an obstacle to the composition of bodies from individual atoms, because if contact from all sides is not possible, then how can a body having length, width, and depth be produced from them? Rather, there would only be scattered atoms, not touching and not possible to touch. And they also agreed on adjacency and composition between that atom and the surrounding atoms, then they differed: al-Ash'ari said: (and the Mu'tazila said: adjacency is the conjunction which is the two atoms being such that they do not...)
His statement: ('out of fear of requiring, etc.') - For if it contacts with one atom, there is one contact subsisting in that atom, not in a part of it, so its division and interpenetration are not required because there is no union in place. However, if it contacts with two atoms, then its contact with another multiplies the locus of the two contacts, so division of the atom is required. But the majority may prevent the entailment of multiplicity of contact by multiplicity of locus. Why is it not permissible for the contact to be multiple according to what it contacts, subsisting in the atom itself without multiplicity in it, like the alignment of the center point with the points assumed on the circumference, for it is multiple with those points at the center without requiring division of the center? And the saying that if the contact is by a part, division occurs, and if by the whole, interpenetration occurs, is a fanciful ruling arising from analogizing the contact of the indivisible to the contact of the divisible. So ponder, for it is subtle.)
His statement: ('and denial') - an indication that the transitive use of 'mukabara' (stubborn denial) with the lam implies the meaning of denial.
His statement: ('for sense, etc.') - i.e., reason after establishing the individual atom by the aid of sense regarding contact. And we only said that the judge is reason because contact is not among the essentially sensible things.
His statement: ('and it is an obstacle') - i.e., the lack of contact understood from his statement: 'he denied that' is an obstacle to the composition of bodies.
His statement: ('how can it be obtained, etc.') - If he means the lack of obtaining length, width, and depth in reality, it is accepted, but on the statement of contact as well, that occurs due to the existence of joints between atoms even if they are touching. For this reason theologians denied magnitude. And if he means the lack of obtaining in sensation, it is denied, for if they are scattered, composition does not occur because it requires necessitating the movement of the other. In it (the objection) there is that it is permissible that that (composition) be by the will of the free agent without contact between them.
His statement: ('out of fear of requiring its division') - And it may be said: division is necessary on the supposition of one atom contacting its like, since the contact of each of them with the other is by a part of it, not all of it, because if one is superimposed on the other such that they unite in position, no body having volume is produced from them. But the necessity of division in the first is apparent, so that some denied it and accepted the second.
His statement: ('for sense bears witness, etc.') - i.e., sensory intuition by means of sensing contact between atoms from the directions bears witness to that, except that he sensed the contact of a single atom with multiple atoms from all sides, and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument regarding the existence of a single atom (jawhar fard) and the conditions of its contact with other atoms. It begins by stating that those who affirm accidents (al-akwan) agreed on the possibility of a single atom surrounded by six atoms touching it from all sides, except for some theologians who denied this. The author then explains that some did not allow the atom to contact more than one atom, fearing it would require the atom to be divided. This is rejected as stubbornness and denial of the sensible, because sense testifies to contact between atoms from all directions. The author argues that if such contact is not possible, then bodies composed of atoms could not have three dimensions; there would only be scattered atoms. Then the author notes that they agreed on adjacency and composition between that atom and the surrounding atoms, but differed on the definition of adjacency: al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila had different views. The commentary then clarifies points: if an atom contacts only one atom, there is only one contact, so no division. But if it contacts two, then multiple contacts could imply division unless we allow multiple contacts without division, like a point aligning with multiple points. The commentary also explains that the judge is reason, not sense, for contact is not essentially sensible. It further refutes an objection that length, width, depth are not obtained even with contact due to joints; and that even without contact, composition could be by divine will. Finally, it notes that sense bears witness to the atom contacting multiple atoms.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
ملاقاة
تجزئة
تلاقي
مجاورة
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An atom cannot contact more than one other atom because that would require the atom to be divided.
Correction: An atom can have multiple contacts without division, as illustrated by the analogy of a single point aligning with many points on a circumference.

Checkpoint
Why did some theologians deny that a single atom can contact more than one atom?
A) Because sense does not show contact from all sides.
B) Because they feared it would require the atom to be divided.
C) Because they denied the existence of atoms.
D) Because they believed bodies are not composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Even indivisible atoms can have multiple contacts without division, analogous to a single point aligning with multiple points on a circumference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evidence that seems to contradict a deeply held assumption, do you react with stubborn denial like those who rejected multi-contact? What would it take for you to submit to truth even when it humbles your intellect?
?
The atom can have multiple contacts without being divided—your heart can be directed toward multiple loves and duties without being fragmented. Is your inner life scattered by conflicting pursuits, or united under the single purpose of seeking Allah?
?
The analogy of a center point aligning with many circumference points shows that unity can coexist with multiplicity. Do you truly believe that Allah's absolute oneness (tawḥīd) is compatible with His many names, actions, and creations? How does your daily life reflect that belief—or fail to?
